In this utterly streamlined, by-the-numbers, Bieber/Perry/Swift music world of today, in which typicality and mediocrity are rewarded, it's hard to imagine a band like Suicide, that was out to break rules and invent new ones as they went along.  As with any other sonic original--- from Stockhausen to Captain Beefheart--- a first listen to Suicide can be downright off-putting: their debut album is grimy, lo-fi, minimalist almost to the point of self-mocking.  Underneath the DGAF trappings, however, is a collection of tight, scary, memorable meditations on modern pop.  Like '70s shock cinema classic \\"Salo, or the 120 Days of Sodom\\", \\"Suicide\\" is not as violent or dirty as reputed, but it is just as disturbing.  Get past the shock value, and you have a valuable work of art.

Not a second is wasted.<br /><br />Yes, there's a certain youthful exuberance, a Zeitgeist, missing. Page seems completely in tune and time with Bonham; together, they seem tighter than the Zep of old. B009INAGY0|5.0|The year was 1990, Death Metal had exploded onto the scene with countless bands trying to outdo each other's goriest lyric or fastest blast beat, etc. Death were already 2 albums in their career and already heraled as a leading band in the scene. So instead of putting out another gore-drenched, squaling lead, blast beated, general death metal album, Chuck Schuldiner put together a masterpiece for the thinking man's metal, a social commentary on the underbelly of society; Spiritual Healing. Instead of writing about the act of abortion, Chuck wrote about the actual issue from a different perspective, and continued touching topics from drug use in pregnancy, religious hypocrisy, human genetics, and public shootings. Apart for the lyrical advancements in the album, the addition of James Murphy on guitar also played a vital role. Up until then, Death had only good or average players, but Murphy could actually shred and write amazing solos and lead parts, which I think really had impact on Chuck's own growth as a player. Along with the great rhythm section of Terry Butler and Bill Andrews, Death had created the perfect storm that would solidfy them as one of the top-dogs of the genre and innovators for future generations, as in today.<br /><br />So once again, Relapse has done a spectacular job reissuing this important album in the Death Metal genre. Out of all the Death albums, this one has been out of print the longest so I imagine alot of newer fans or some old ones (who may have lost or worn out their old Combat records one) may be looking to get a copy. Well, I can say with full confidence: this is the definitive version of Spiritual Healing. The remastering is crisp and pristine, no bottom end was lost or suffered and the guitars shine bright. It is well worth the upgrade from the orginal CD. The bonus tracks are really great for the more hardcore fans, but I think any fan of Death Metal in general would enjoy hearing the evolution of what would become such an important piece of Death and Death Metal history. Most notably, at the end of disc 2's tracks, we get a glimpse of Chuck's influences from French metal bands in 'Jon A Qua', in which Chuck sings at almost Rob Halford pitch! The booklet has some great and informative liner notes from James Murphy, Terry Butler, Michael Schon Poulsen of Volbeat and Chuck's sister, Beth Schuldiner. Also included are some great photo's from this era of Death,a very cool peliminary sketch of the album artwork and the lyrics of course. If you're big Death fan, I suggest getting the 3-Disc version at Relapse's home site, which features a live show from 1990, which has pretty ok quality(c'mon it was 1990 for cryin' out loud!), but even greater, features Pre-Human rehearsal takes, which is very interesting and cool to hear. Overall, if you're a fan of Death or Death Metal and somehow don't know or missed out on this album, here's a perfect opportunity to experience a vital part of what Death Metal is today. Let the metal Flow!tant piece of Death and Death Metal history. B009186E3O|5.0|I have been collecting records since the summer of 1965 which coincidentally is just about the time that The Rolling Stones were in Dublin, Ireland playing before a frenzied audience of teenagers at the Adelphi Theater.  Down through the decades it has always been my contention that The Rolling Stones made their very best music during their days at London Records in the 1960's.  So when offered the chance to take a look at the new box set from Abkco \\"The Rolling Stones: Charlie Is My Darling -- Ireland 1965\\"  I jumped at it. This unique package features the long-forgotten 65 minute film by Peter Whitehead \\"Charley Is My Darling\\" which was recorded in its entirety during that marvelous summer weekend so many years ago.  The footage has been lovingly restored and both DVD and Blu-ray versions are included.  Also featured are two marvelous audio CD's, a 10\\" vinyl album, a colorful replica tour poster and an amazing 44 page hardcover book with sensational photos, newspaper clippings and the narrative of just went down that weekend. Trust me, if you are a fan of the band this one is a keeper!<br /><br />For me the most surprising thing about this package was the film.  Ordinarily, I am not a big fan of this sort of thing but I found \\"Charlie Is My Darling\\" to be quite entertaining indeed.  It was fun watching the boys as they traverse the Irish countryside on a train enroute to their gig in Dublin.  As you might expect Mick Jagger has some very interesting things to say and there are numerous opportunities for the other band members to chime in as well.  It is amazing to me that even at this very early stage of their career how little privacy the guys have and they talk about that.  There are interesting shots of the Irish countryside as the train chugs along and they are plenty of shots of the boys horsing around.  Lots of fun!  But clearly the best part of \\"Charlie Is My Darling\\" is the footage from the concert.  The raw energy is simply unbelievable!  See if you agree.<br /><br />Now I would certainly concur with another reviewer who felt that the \\"two audio CD's are the real gem here\\".  These discs are positively out of this world.  Disc One is the soundtrack from the film and features \\"live\\" versions of such Rolling Stones classics as \\"Pain In My Heart\\",  \\"The Last Time\\", \\"Time Is On My Side\\" and of course \\"(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction\\" which was riding high on the charts all over the world when the film was being made.  Also feature in the soundtrack are the original studio recordings of \\"Heart of Stone\\" and \\"Play With Fire\\".  Meanwhile, there are a number of other terrific tunes in this 22 song soundtrack.  Among my favorites is the boys doing an impromptu version of an old London folk anthem \\"Maybe It's Because I'm A Londoner\\" which was a big hit in England in the late 1940's.  The second audio disc \\"The Rolling Stones Live In England '65\\" features a bakers dozen of outstanding performances from the group's performances in their native land during that incredible year.  There are some really interesting tunes on this one including the band's take on such diverse tunes as Willie Dixon's \\"Little Red Rooster\\", Hank Snow's \\"I'm Moving On\\" and of all things Bobby Troup's great jazz standard \\"Route 66\\".  Who'da thunk it?<br /><br />The bottom line here is that the \\"Charlie Is My Darling:  Ireland 1965\\" box set is outstanding in every way.  It is clear to me that a lot of thought and love went into producing this package.  This is a collection that can be enjoyed by fans of the band, collectors and general audiences alike.  Furthermore, due to the historical significance of this box set I am quite sure that many of these will wind up in libraries as well.  Very highly recommended!.<br /><br />Now I would certainly concur with another reviewer who felt that the \\"two audio CD's are the real gem here\\".  These discs are positively out of this world.  Disc One is the soundtrack from the film and features \\"live\\" versions of such Rolling Stones classics as \\"Pain In My Heart\\",  \\"The Last Time\\", \\"Time Is On My Side\\" and of course \\"(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction\\" which was riding high on the charts all over the world when the film was being made. 0316212377|4.0|Iain M Banks' Culture novels have been around for 25 years now.  Given Mr. Banks propensity for experimenting with the form and content of the things, they've been a bit hit and miss... though given his skill as an author, even the misses are thought provoking and well worth reading.  The Hydrogen Sonata is at the faster paced, lighter end of the spectrum and I had a rollicking good time reading it.  The title doesn't have a lot to do with the content,  the heroine seems to be a pawn, the Minds remain inscrutable, the ending is morally ambiguous, and you can't help wondering while you read it if the chief protagonists are all in pursuit of a MacGuffin - but the action set pieces are fantastic, the environments epic and the themes of the nature of Memory, Machiavelli and Truth suitably imponderable.  In short, a Culture novel.  If you haven't read one before... choose some of the earlier stuff to get started on.  If you have, then you know more or less what you're in for.

B005NJU8PA|5.0|In interest of full disclosure, before you read this review, I should mention that I am featured in a chapter and mentioned elsewhere, and that Tim is a friend of mine. Having said that, both the review and the book are worth reading.<br /><br />No, to business: the 4-Hour Chef is described as a cookbook for people who don't buy cookbooks. I would say that is a slight misnomer, especially in my case. For me, it's been a cookbook for people who don't USE cookbooks.<br /><br />I buy plenty of cookbooks, generally those that are written by celebrity chefs and pertain to either steak or cake, these being the two foods I most enjoy eating (and don't even get me started on a cake made of steak--I hope that's Tim's next project). I read them and enjoy them, but I almost never USE them. If I get one usable recipe out a cookbook, it's a lot. Still, I never consider it money wasted, because cookbooks like nice on the shelf, and to me books are as much for decoration as they for utility and pleasure.<br /><br />Tim's book serves all three of these purposes.<br /><br />Unlike most cookbooks that just give you ingredients and directions, the 4-Hour Chef doen'st just TELL you how to took; it TEACHES you how to cook. Since getting the book two weeks ago (advance review copy, I have made three recipes: the salt baked sweet potatoes (easy and delish), the go-carb yeast waffles (holy crap amazing) and the bacon infused bourbon (had to. as good as it sounds). Of course, my copy is now getting beat up and has a few stains on it, but, you know, that's the price of glory and all.<br /><br />I decided to actually try these recipes because not only was there progression in terms of difficulty, but because Tim actually explained the science of how they were made; learning about everything infusion to gels to powders--this is the stuff that made me WANT to try these recipes. Of course, they all looked and sounded delicious, but I really just enjoyed the process of learning.<br /><br />And that's what 4HC is really about: learning. And more specifically, learning how to learn. Learning the skill of learning skills; or, as Tim dubs it, \\"meta-learning.\\"<br /><br />If you're familiar with Tim's work, you know that cooking isn't his thing. And if you read the 4-Hour Workweek and the 4-Hour Body, you know that neither task and time management or fitness are really his \\"thing\\" either; he wrote about those things BECAUSE of his thing: breaking down complex skills and learning them quickly.<br /><br />As I see it, the 4 Hour Chef is Tim at his most \\"naked.\\" Since 2007, when 4HWW came out, we've heard Tim talk about learning tango in 4 weeks, or learning how to swim, competing and medaling in Chinese kickboxing with very little time to train--or any of the other accomplishments that he's become well known for. But we've never SEEN him learn these thing in real time; we always get the stories long after the fact.<br /><br />While all of the feats in both 4HWW and 4HB are impressive by any measure, there is something very different in this book. In the 4-Hour Workweek, Tim was focusing on his job. While he wrote some revolutionary stuff, ultimately you'd EXPECT a Princeton grad to be smart and good at his job. In the 4-Hour Body, Tim is writing about fitness, a hobby of his for nearly 20 years. Again, some very smart and revolutionary stuff in there, but after 20 years, you would expect him to know what he's talking about.<br /><br />That's what makes 4HC completely different: Tim is learning as we learn.<br /><br />Prior to this book, Tim was not a cook. It's not even that he wasn't a \\"good cook\\" -- he just didn't cook at all. (Evidence: his youtube video of making eggs in a microwave.)<br /><br />At 34-years old, Ferriss went into this project with NO skills--and in about 6 months, came out on the other side of the ordeal being more proficient in the kitchen than many people are after 20 years of cooking for their families. I'm sure Tim isn't a master chef, buthe learned an incredibly complex series of skills and built his proficiency to a high level in a very short period of time. And that, after all, if his THING.<br /><br />For the first time, Tim isn't just telling stories; he's taking the reader along for the ride. And because he wants the reader to be able to do what he does and learn as he learns, the first part of the book gets you read to do exactly what he does: learn.<br /><br />The first section of 4HC is called \\"META\\" and in that section reveals his strategies for learning, and creates and lays out an entire framework for learning any skill or becoming extremely proficient at just about anything in six months. Whether it's shooting hoops or speaking Spanish--yes--learning to cook, the section on Meta-learning along is worth the price of the book. And, in fact, is the real POINT of the book.<br /><br />From my perspective, cooking is the vehicle of demonstration; it's the particular skillset that Tim chose to illustrate both meta-learning and it's benefits. The recipes are fantastic, the skills he covers are valuable, and the education in the culinary arts and culinary world are invaluable. So it the nutrition information.<br /><br />But learning. Learning is where it's at.<br /><br />And this book will help you learn anything. But first, you must learn how to learn.<br /><br />If you haven't already picked this up, do so. It's worth the read, worth the time, and certainly worth the money.he learned an incredibly complex series of skills and built his proficiency to a high level in a very short period of time. And that, after all, if his THING.<br /><br />For the first time, Tim isn't just telling stories; he's taking the reader along for the ride. And because he wants the reader to be able to do what he does and learn as he learns, the first part of the book gets you read to do exactly what he does: learn.<br /><br />The first section of 4HC is called \\"META\\" and in that section reveals his strategies for learning, and creates and lays out an entire framework for learning any skill or becoming extremely proficient at just about anything in six months. Whether it's shooting hoops or speaking Spanish--yes--learning to cook, the section on Meta-learning along is worth the price of the book. And, in fact, is the real POINT of the book.<br /><br />From my perspective, cooking is the vehicle of demonstration; it's the particular skillset that Tim chose to illustrate both meta-learning and it's benefits. The recipes are fantastic, the skills he covers are valuable, and the education in the culinary arts and culinary world are invaluable. So it the nutrition information.<br /><br />But learning. Learning is where it's at.<br /><br />And this book will help you learn anything. But first, you must learn how to learn.<br /><br />If you haven't already picked this up, do so. It's worth the read, worth the time, and certainly worth the money.

0060555661|5.0|By Juan Felipe Gers<br /><br />The Path to become an Intelligent Investor<br /><br />The Intelligent Investor by Benjamin Graham is perhaps the Bible of Value Investing, and one of the first books a person learning about the investments world picks up. Perhaps up until this point you have never heard of the book, or the author, or the pivotal concept which is basically the point of the book. If not implied in the first sentence, I add my name to the thousands, perhaps millions of people that have read it and recommend it as a cornerstone of investing, not only for the beginning investor, but for all types and levels of investors. Besides a recommendation for reading it, I hope that the following words will help you get a best summary of the book and the important concepts it brings with it.<br /> Not by any account is it an easy book to read (at least for the early investor like myself), nor is it a discouraging one. The edition I ready came with a great prologue from John C. Bogle, founder of the Vanguard Group, that greatly facilitated going through the book by basically introducing some of the concepts and important sentences of the book in a simple way to give you somewhat of a foundation of what to expect. I hope you can get this edition or something similar to it.<br /> It is worth mentioning that the richest man in the world a few years back, nowadays the third richest, is an advocate of Benjamin Graham and Value Investing, stating his investment strategy is \\"85% Graham, 15% Fisher (another great investor of the past century)\\". Graham's popularity is in many ways due to this man, Warren Buffet, once his student at Columbia University. To motivate you to read this book, Buffet once stated that four of his most favorite and beloved books were written before 1950: The Wealth of Nations by Adam Smith (where the concept of Capitalism was mainly introduced), Common Stocks and Uncommon Profits by Philip Fisher, and Security Analysis and The Intelligent Investor by Benjamin Graham. This brings us back to discussing The Intelligent Investor<br /> The book has in some respects a good order or structure. It introduces the aim of the book and the reader, introducing some ideas, and then going on to describe the type of investors. First starting with the defensive investor and what he should do, he then moves on to what the active investor should not do and then what he should do, later explaining some strategies for him/her. He cites some examples, then talks about the role of the investor as a business owner, and finally closes the book with the concept of margin of safety, the basis for value investing.<br />Graham is perhaps philosophical in many aspects throughout the book, easily grasping this in the introduction of the book. He states here that the market has historically gone down, then up, then back down again, then up, etc., so before doing any kind of investment, se historical trends and figures, and all sorts of financial of operational history of the investment (i.e. the company). He introduces the concept of going against the current and public sentiment, having a \\"margin of safety\\" and a sound investment philosophy. He then warns about doing a thorough analysis of the investment, or else the action would be \\"speculation\\".<br /> A key factor in value investing is to understand public sentiment and being mentally ready to both go against the current and having short-term loses. As long as your investment has been well analyzed and justified, you should be tranquil. The author states that what matters are the Values, not the price movements. This goes along with advisers. The most important piece of knowledge is the analysis of the investor and not that of a third party. Many times advisers will benefit themselves even though their customer doesn't benefit, so be wary, very selective, and get counseling primarily as a second opinion. An important relevant sentence Graham wrote stated \\"Much advice is given free.\\"<br /> Inthe book two types of investors are introduced: defensive and aggressive investors. I will talk very briefly about what these mean to not spoil the book (and make this summary more painful than what it might feel). The defensive are generally highly risk averse, which means they prefer less price variation and a lower expected return of the investment to reduce the riskiness of the investment. Thus he/she will buy primarily US Bonds (the safest investment out there), along with securities in large and prominent well-known companies. This type of investment will be the basis for the enterprising investor, from which he will diversify into secondary stocks and corporate bonds. It is perhaps an overstatement, but this type of investor will make a very thorough analysis of his investment and buy the stock at a lower price than what he thinks is its real value (value investing). Low markets and extreme negative popularity will make for great investment opportunities, as well as a low dividend rate.<br /> There is perhaps no better sentence to depict the theory of value investing than what Graham quoted: \\"the last shall be first and the first shall be last\\". In investment this comes up in trends, with public buying when everybody buys and selling when everybody sells, opening up space for over-valuation or a security being undervalued, and with the correct analysis and historical knowledge the investor can beat the market. This is where the concept of margin of safety starts, where the basis forms. It continues with relevant diversification of the portfolio (buying different companies ideally in different industries, buying diversified bonds and stocks and in today's world this list in other countries). Diversifying, buying in low markets and selling in high markets, thus margin of safety, and sticking to this concept of value investing, will not assure you a positive return or a great return, but it will increase your probabilities and most likely will, especially withmore study, experience. Like Graham stated at the end and which gives a great summary of what this books transmits: \\"To achieve satisfactory returns is easier than most people realize; to achieve superior results is harder than it looks.\\"<br /> I hope that this summary has helped you get the feel for what The Intelligent Investor is about and help you towards your path into the investment world. I also hope that you decide to read it and expand your ideas of investment and the different strategies. Perhaps you will want to get deeply intrigued by the concept of value investing and decide to read Security Analysis and other books of this sort, but also making sure you read other investment philosophies. Finally, I hope I have helped the next Warren Buffet with summarizing what is possibly the most important book in value investing.

B009682EFQ|5.0|First I'd like to say that I'm a big fan of Neurosis, but I haven't always been and don't fully enjoy all of their work (mostly the really early stuff).  I've seen them live twice and I didn't have any knowledge of the band before seeing them.  They were opening for other bands.  The last time they came thru my town was when they released 'Through Silver In Blood' back around 1996.  I didn't know the music but was absolutely blown away by their stage performance.  They're the type of band that can make you feel stoned when your sober.  Their music is deep and hypnotic.  The general formula for their albums is that they flow from song to song.  Light flowing instrumentals and effects flow into crushing guitar solos while the vocals are raw and dark, yet full of atmospheric and image conjuring words and poetry.  They seem to make an album as one whole piece - not broken down for radio consumption - and I respect that as it is the more artistic thing to do.  Their songs flow from almost overwhelmingly heavy apocalyptic doom filled rage and despair to light airy instrumentals that break up the songs into individual pieces of work.<br /><br />  Now, if your familiar with this band and you've enjoyed the direction they're going then don't hesitate another minute and buy it!  It's easily as good or better than past releases in my opinion.  They aren't straying far from their recent formula which is good in my opinion yet it is totally different and unique.  Nothing on this release feels like it's been done before.<br /><br />  If you aren't familiar with this band, this is my own quick and dirty interpretation but this is how I've come to view the music.  They seemed to start out with some very heavy punk roots.  I didn't particularly enjoy their first cd's.  I didn't really get into this band until 'Through Silver In Blood' and if your looking to get to know their music I would suggest starting with their latest releases and working backwards through their catalog.  The newest music is easiest to digest and the farther you look back the harder and grittier the music becomes - much less polished.<br /><br />This is a band that has evolved into something that is just amazing to me and why the rest of the world either can't see it or can't handle it is beyond me.  When I hear their music, I get images of Leonard Baskin's art, of Native American religion and folklore, of images of our own demise and doom individually and as a whole, I get images of doom, death, birth, light and dark, the elements.  I could go on and on.  It's dark and it's a beautiful thing to experience.<br />If you enjoy deep moving music, check these guys out.  And do them justice by listening on a quality system with no distractions or use headphones.  You'll be rewarded.  Their music will unfold to you as you hear it again and again growing on you revealing more with each repetition.

Mrs. Moore and her future daughter-in-law, Adela Quested, are captivated by the country on their visit and want to see more of the real India. Unfortunately, they get more than they bargained for during a planned visit to the Marabar Caves outside the city, which leads to misunderstandings and courtroom drama that changes lives forever.<br /><br />A Passage to India is E.M. Forster's most famous novel and for good reason. The book says a great deal about British attitudes toward the Indian culture and people during the 1920's. Forster portrays a love-hate relationship among key players. The difference in cultures and lack of understanding is superbly drawn, as is the barely concealed disdain between the British and locals. You can almost feel the simmering animosity threatening to erupt into a full explosion.<br /><br />The book is fairly long at 421 pages, yet I wished I'd known more about how secondary characters dealt with the aftermath of the trial. Forster barely mentions some of the characters afterward, which is a shame. Still, this is my favorite of his collection, and well worth reading.

0618918248|3.0|I have organized my review of The God Delusion into two sections: a general review of the book and specific critiques of Dawkins' arguments.  Richard Dawkins is a brilliant scientist and an excellent writer (see his book \\"The Selfish Gene\\" for proof), however, to me this book reflects more of an attempt on his part to settle a political score than a serious attempt at philosophy in debating the existence of God.  The reason I say this is because nearly all of the arguments Dawkins makes to refute the existence of God are either overgeneralizations, selective abstractions of evidence, or straw man arguments.  Dawkins, as much as anyone, should be the first to recognize that these primitive rhetorical tactics are just as potent in refuting the scientific worldview as they are the theistic worldview, and hence, it would behoove him not to use them.<br /><br />GENERAL REVIEW:<br />Insofar as there is still a need to debunk the myth of God as the omniscient and omnipotent \\"old man in the sky,\\" as suggested by a literal interpretation of the Old Testament, Dawkins does an excellent job of debunking this myth and invalidating the merits of a literal interpretation of the Bible by using historical and scientific evidence, as well as sound reasoning.  He is meticulous in explaining how such a view of God is inconsistent with an objective scientific worldview and he also does a good job of highlighting inconsistencies within the Bible itself, as well as the hypocrisy of some of the religious fundamentalists who critique him.<br /><br />However, the main strength of this book is also its main weakness.  The anthropomorphic representation of God that is propagated by fundamentalists - one that depicts God as being jealous and vengeful, like humans, but yet omnipotent and capable of intervening in all human affairs (including football games) - has been debunked for centuries, as evidenced by the quotes Dawkins himself provides from Thomas Jefferson and other American founding fathers.  Not only to do most educated people today reject this representation of God, but many theists and deists do as well.  Though some holdovers still remain who believe in this version of God, it seems that Dawkins truly believes that his rational arguments can convince these people that they are wrong.  This is as much a waste of time as it is trying to convince those who believe the world is flat that it is actually round.  George Will has a great line about the effort to try and convince irrational people of rational things:  \\"It is impossible to reason people out of affiliations they have not been reasoned into.\\"<br /><br />So Dawkins rightly debunks the mythical, anthropomorphic God of religious fundamentalists, but then he uses their inane arguments to set them up as straw men representing the entire theistic worldview.  And then after he defeats these straw men (as easily as the Harlem Globetrotters used to defeat the Washington Generals) he parlays this victory into a validation of his atheistic worldview.  Dawkins (and every intelligent atheist) well knows that it is equally impossible to prove the inexistence of God as it is to prove that God exists.  As such, rather than providing convincing evidence to support his case directly (that God does not exist), he smartly sets out to debunk the most indefensible positions of his opponents and then he uses logic to convince the reader that if their anthropomorphic representation of God cannot possibly exist, then his opposing view (that God does not exist) must certainly be true.  To me, this is flawed logic and it is beneath someone as intelligent and accomplished as Richard Dawkins.<br /><br />Furthermore, Dawkins has entitled his book The God Delusion, suggesting the book provides a critique of a general belief in God, and on page 57 he supports this by saying \\"I am not attacking any particular version of God or gods.  I am attacking God, all gods... wherever and whenever they have been or will be invented.\\"  Yet a few pages earlier (on pg. 41) Dawkins writes: \\"My title, The God Delusion, does not refer to the God of Einstein... I am talking only about supernatural gods, of which the most familiar to the majority of my readers will be Yahweh, the God of the Old Testament.\\"  Notice that Dawkins makes an example of the concrete, easily falsifiable version of God of the Abrahamic religions (Judaism, Christianity and Islam), Yahweh, and he does not make any mention in the book of the abstract, immanent representations of God that come from the Eastern religions, like Hinduism and Jainism.  In these religions, God is believed to be an aspect of Self - one that is not separate from humans but hidden within each of us.  This notion of God is much trickier for Dawkins to disprove, which is why he doesn't touch it.  That's fine, but if he only intended to invalidate the God figure of Abrahamic fundamentalists (whether He be called Yahweh or Allah), then he should not have chosen a title suggesting a more broad invalidation of theism.<br />---------------------------------------------------<br /><br />SPECIFIC CRITIQUES:<br />1) Dawkins' Estimates of Probability of God's Existence:<br />While in the process of highlighting his differences with agnostic philosopher TH Huxley, Dawkins suggests that while science \\"can neither prove nor disprove the existence of something does not put existence and non-existence on an even footing (pg. 72).\\"  He then goes on to suggest that while, technically, the existence of God is unknowable, he is quite certain that the probability of God's existence is so small that it might as well be zero.  However, in order to estimate the probability of something, you have to have a reasonable idea of the parameters of its context.  For instance, in order to estimate the probability that a tossed die will land on the number 6, you have to know how many sides the die has, whether it is a fair or weighted die, and it would help to know a few other environmental factors as well.  But it is reasonably within the scope of human ability to calculate or estimate these parameters, and thus we can establish a reasonably accurate probability for the occurrence of a tossed die landing on the number 6.  But when it comes to estimating the probability of God's existence, there are too many unknown and unknowable parameters to make any kind of a meaningful estimate about its probability.  What science has been able to document and measure about the universe is equivalent to a grain of sand on a beach a thousand miles long.  If you don't believe me, ask the physicists who study dark matter, dark energy and quantum entanglement, as well as the mathematicians who have studied the Turing Halting Problem and Goedel's Incompleteness Theorems.  Hence, Dawkins' estimation of the probability of God's existence being \\"very low [lower than 50 percent] but short of zero (pg. 73)\\" is little more than an arbitrary estimation without any significance.<br /><br />2) Doing Good.<br />There isn't a serious theologian that I know who believes that atheists are incapable of the same types of moral behavior as theists, simply because they do not believe in God.  Most reasonable theologians would agree that atheists are every bit as capable as theists of generosity and selflessness, and that, conversely, a belief in God does not necessarily predispose an individual to moral behavior.  In fact, history is replete with examples of those who, despite calling themselves \\"religious,\\" have committed abominable acts of violence - often in the name of religion - and most theists would concede this to be true.  But \\"doing good\\" is not a black-and-white issue, and it can be argued that there may be quantitative and qualitative differences in the \\"good\\" that is done by theists and atheists.<br /><br />In Chapter 6, Dawkins offers the following evolutionary hypotheses to explain why we are compelled by our genes to do good, independent of religious influence: 1)Altruism towards our offspring increases the likelihood of their survival, and thus the reproduction of our genes; 2) Doing good for others compels them to do good for us, which helps to ensure our own survival so that we can pass on our genes; and 3) The Potlatch Effect, which suggests that there is competition within social groups for the status of being regarded as \\"the most generous\\" in society because this projects our social (and genetic) dominance to the rest of society.  While these explanations provided by Dawkins are all reasonable and may all be true, none of them explain the types of altruistic behavior that many religious people engage in, including: forgiving those who have harmed you and \\"turning the other cheek;\\" practicing random, secret acts of kindness to strangers; and sacrificing one's life in benevolent martyrdom to save those who are genetically unrelated.<br /><br />To be sure, atheists are not only capable these types of selfless acts, but regularly perform them, unimpeded by their lack of a belief in God: however, there are a few caveats.  First, as Dawkins suggests earlier in the book, all atheists have been raised within the context of a religious culture, from which none of us can escape, and thus they have all been imprinted with religious philosophy.  Dawkins actually notes that among atheists in Ireland there is a running joke in which they often ask each other whether one is a \\"Catholic atheist\\" or a \\"Protestant atheist.\\"  Given the inescapable presence of religion in society, as well as the imprinting we all receive, how can Dawkins, or anyone else, be sure that the selfless, moral acts of atheists are not actually inspired by the religious imprinting they received from their various cultural influences?<br /><br />Second, it is reasonable to believe that religious groups provide their adherents with more reminders to engage in moral behavior than what secular society provides to atheists (or to any of us), particularly when it comes to engaging in moral behavior that has no direct benefit to them or the reproduction of their genes.  Religious life, unlike secular life, is based on a metaphysical system of lending: give unto others now (of your time, your possessions, your money, and even your life) and get repaid with interest (1000% if you believe the Christian Scriptures) in the next life, or for all eternity.  Hence, since religious adherents get more frequent reminders to do good, and also because they would seem to have greater incentive to do good (i.e., the prospect of eternal dividends), it is reasonable to argue that religious adherents may have more factors predisposing them to do good, more often, than do atheists (though atheists will argue, and rightly so, that their moral behavior is not tainted by the prospect of gaining a personal reward, and thus it is more pure).<br /><br />There is actually some evidence to support this, and this evidence was cited a couple of years ago by the New York Times columnist, Nicholas Kristof, in an article entitled \\"Bleeding Heart Tightwads.\\"  In the article, Kristof admits to trying to \\"shame [fellow] liberals into being more charitable\\" because, according to the data he cites, religious conservatives are twice as generous as secular liberals, even after controlling for income.<br /><br />But beyond the discussion of potential differences between theists and atheists in moral behavior, there is also reason to believe that the benevolent, enlightened societal goals of secular liberals may only be possible with significant contributions from religious adherents.  This is not my assessment, but the assessment of the famous German philosopher, and an atheist's atheist, Jurgen Habermas, in his book \\"An Awareness of What is Missing: Faith and Reason in a Post-secular Age,\\" which was summarized by Stanley Fish of the New York Times.  In the book, Habermas says:<br /><br />\\"Among the modern societies, only those that are able to introduce into the secular domain the essential contents of their religious traditions which point beyond the merely human realm will also be able to rescue the substance of the human... [as enlightenment rational morality] is aimed at the insight of individuals, and does not foster any impulse toward solidarity, that is, toward morally guided collective action.\\"  As such, Habermas laments that in the face of large-scale injustices (like genocide in Darfur or segregation in the United States), secular enlightenment intellectuals do not have \\"sufficient strength to awaken, and to keep awake, in the minds of secular subjects, an awareness of the violations of solidarity throughout the world, an awareness of what is missing, of what cries out to heaven.\\"<br /><br />This awareness is best exemplified by John Newton, the 18th century slave trader turned abolitionist (and atheist turned Christian) who, through the study of Christian theology later in life, came to renounce his profession and join in solidarity with the African slaves he once sold, leading him to write the famous hymn, Amazing Grace.  Once again, no reasonable person would argue that a benevolent atheist would not have acted as John Newton had.  However, just as an individual is more likely to exercise if he has a treadmill in his bedroom, I don't think it's unreasonable to believe that the regular reminders that religious adherents get - to examine their behavior (in the confession booth or anywhere else); challenge their selfish beliefs; and overcome their complacency - increases their likelihood of engaging in generous, selfless behavior.<br /><br />3) Qualifying Statements:<br />When individuals that Dawkins respects (e.g., Einstein or Stephen Jay Gould) are quoted in the book with statements that are dissonant with his atheistic arguments, Dawkins either explains them away as them being taken out of context or as being the unreliable consequence of a panicky deathbed conversion to theism: in other words, don't believe thesestatements because they are not valid representations of how the individual in question REALLY felt.  However, Dawkins does not extend the same benefit of the doubt to those individuals he does not respect (e.g., Rev. Fulton Sheen and Dr. Francis Collins).  When he quotes these individuals, he assumes that the written public record of their statements is an accurate reflection of how these people REALLY felt, and thus there is no need to qualify their statements with anything others, nor is there a need to provide any nuance in interpretation.  It strikes me as more than coincidental that Dawkins' allies always seem to get misinterpreted (and thus need him to contextualize or qualify their dissonant statements with others that depict how they REALLY feel), but yet Dawkins opponents always seem to say what they mean and mean what they say, and thus they don't deserve the benefit of him contextualizing or qualifying their statements.<br /><br />In either case, we can never know what a person truly means when they speak and this is especially true if we have no information about the context in which a person's statements were made.  We are all politicians, and scientists are no different.  Whenever we speak, the meaning of what we say is co-created by our actual statements and the context in which we make them.  There are many reasons why people may say things that betray their true feelings, including the need to sell something (like a book); or to drum up support for a particular cause (like a political campaign); or for social popularity; or simply to gain publicity for its own sake.  Dawkins understands this, which is why he tries to correct the record when it comes to those whom he believes are his allies in atheism.<br /><br />Hence, when Einstein says that \\"Science without religion is lame, religion without science is blind\\" and \\"God doesn't play dice with the world\\" - which are quotes that theists exploit to suggest that Einstein believed in God as they do - Dawkins provides other quotes that contradict these to demonstrate that these theistic statements by Einstein do not represent how he REALLY felt.  Similarly, when Dawkins discusses Stephen Jay Gould's argument in his book, Rocks of Ages, that science and religion have comparable jurisdiction over separate but equal realms of existence, Dawkins offers the following assessment: \\"I simply do not believe that Gould could possibly have meant much of what he wrote in Rocks of Ages (pg. 81).\\"  He then provides other quotes by Gould to invalidate these statements because they muddle his case against the existence of God.  But how does Dawkins really know how Einstein felt about God (I assume they never met, as Dawkins is likely too young to have ever met Einstein) and on what basis does he have to suggest that Stephen Jay Gould purposely betrayed his true feelings about religion in his own book?

B008Y1YK0I|5.0|I first remember seeing \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\" in 1971 when a bunch of us college students went to see the cut-up(actually butchered) version of it in a small theater. I may have seen it when it first came out because I still have the movie program but I don't remember(I was 11 or 12). I was more into James Bond and Ray Harryhausen films than three hour epics. However, I do remember going with some people that I worked with to see the restored version in 1989 at the Cinema 21 in Mission Valley. We were spoiled in San Diego because we had not one, but six stand-alone movie theatres that could show 70mm films like \\"Lawrence\\". Sadly, four have been torn down while the other two were turned into a Staples and Bookstar(respectively). I mention this because you don't get to experience the real impact of \\"Lawrence\\" unless you see it in a theatre that can handle 70mm films. In fact, the last 70mm film I saw was Kenneth Branagh's version of \\"Hamlet\\"(1996) and prior to that was Ron Howard's \\"Far and Away'(1992). They just don't make films in this format anymore. Lean used it one more time for \\"Ryan's Daughter\\"(1970) but he he went back to 35mm for \\"Doctor Zhivago\\"(1965) and \\"A Passage to India\\"(1984). I mention all this because going to the movies, at least when I was young, was an event. I still remember seeing \\"The Ten Commandments\\"(1956) and \\"Ben-Hur\\"(1959) and being taken away by the scope of the production. With this new restored version of \\"Lawrence\\" finally making it to Blu Ray at least everyone can enjoy what it was like seeing it for the first time(aspect rato of 2:20:1)even though it's on a flat screen TV. Once you get carried away with the scope of the film, it feels like you're seeing it on a large screen. This Blu Ray is stunning to say the least and the restoration team at Sony deserves at lot of credit for the hard work they put into it just in time for it's 50th Anniversary. Having been to many of the locations in Jordan and Spain where Lean filmed most of the picture, I can honestly say that this Blu Ray feels like you're actually there. I'm not going to go into detail because there are other reviewers who can cover that better than me, but if you're a serious collector of films then you need to have this in your collection. David Lean's films are perfect subjects for Blu Ray and with this release all of his films since \\"Bridge on the River Kwai\\"(1957) until \\"A Passage to India\\"(his last in 1984) have now been issued on Blu Ray. The only exception is \\"Ryan's Daughter\\" and hopefully Warner Home Video will get around to that in the future. This Sony release of \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\" is definately on my list for one of the top Blu Ray releases of 2012 along with Paramount's \\"To Catch a Thief\\", Criteron's \\"Rosemary's Baby\\", Warner's \\"Empire of the Sun\\" and Universal's \\"Classic Monsters Box Set\\". For those who love great filmmaking, enjoy \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\" in all it's Blu Ray glory.

It's the entire premise for a family film: (one-hundred and-) 18-year-old daughter Mavis (Selena Gomez) wants to see the world but her father Dracula (Adam Sandler) is terrified of humanity after his wife was killed by an angry human mob.  Humans in this world are the real monsters - you know, like \\"Monsters, Inc.\\" only with more traditional critters from horror movie canon.<br /><br />In honor of Mavis' upcoming birthday, all the family friends have been invited: Frankenstein's Monster (Kevin James) and his bride (Fran Drescher); a chubby mummy that's obviously based on the oft auto-tuned voice of Cee Lo Green; harried werewolf parents Wayne (Steve Buscemi) and Wanda (Molly Shanon); Griffin the invisible man (David Spade); and a litany of blink-and-you'll-miss it monster cameos. Drac decides to manipulate his daughter into staying at the hotel by terrifying her with an elaborate deception.  Then clueless traveling human hipster Johnny (Adam Sandberg) stumbles into the castle and suddenly Mavis' world is upended by evidence that humanity may not be that bad after all.<br /><br />The plot bumps along unevenly. Never mind that nearly every single main character was arguably human at one point - Frankenstein's Monster is made of humans (a running joke to explain how Johnny, disguised as another Monster, is distantly related), Dracula was human, the werewolves are presumably human some of the time, Griffin is still human, the mummy was...you get the idea.  Quasimodo is the chief antagonist (Jon Lovitz), but last I checked he was human too.<br /><br />The plot pivots on the \\"zing\\" - love at first sight, basically - but that concept is introduced before the big reveal of a gift from Mavis' mother that only reinforces what we knew all along about \\"zinging.\\" The idea that my little girl might fall in love with the first man she meets also rubs me the wrong way...Mavis can't just explore the world on her own, she has to fall in love with a man (sparing her father the horror of dating!) so she transitions from protective father figure to protective boyfriend.<br /><br />There's evidence of severe rewrites all over this script - since it's been in development since 2006 perhaps this is not surprising. It all culminates in a series of reveals and betrayals, which are resolved by our protagonists going on a road trip to reunite the two wayward souls.  Or at least it seems that way, until you realize that none of the supporting characters actually do anything at all.<br /><br />\\"Hotel Transylvania\\" regularly flirts with the difficult realities of monsters - at one point, Drac says, \\"I can't kill him, it would set monsters back hundreds of years.\\" But this is a PG flick, so even the notion of vampirism isn't considered, which is a shame.  It's probably the only way Mavis and Johnny will ever truly be together.<br /><br />Despite my qualms about the plot, this is a light-hearted love note to fans of monster movies and...their daughters. \\"Hotel Transylvania\\" hopes you fondly recall the horror movies of the past but not too much lest you remember that even vampires have to eat.  It's basically a paranormal romance disguised as a kid's movie..you get the idea.  Quasimodo is the chief antagonist (Jon Lovitz), but last I checked he was human too.<br /><br />The plot pivots on the \\"zing\\" - love at first sight, basically - but that concept is introduced before the big reveal of a gift from Mavis' mother that only reinforces what we knew all along about \\"zinging.\\" The idea that my little girl might fall in love with the first man she meets also rubs me the wrong way...Mavis can't just explore the world on her own, she has to fall in love with a man (sparing her father the horror of dating!) so she transitions from protective father figure to protective boyfriend.<br /><br />There's evidence of severe rewrites all over this script - since it's been in development since 2006 perhaps this is not surprising. It all culminates in a series of reveals and betrayals, which are resolved by our protagonists going on a road trip to reunite the two wayward souls. 145162445X|1.0|This is easily among the worst books I have ever read in a subject that is interesting to me.  The text is somewhat disorganized, flavorlessly written without any kind of style, and the presentation is just poor.  Among other things, one of the problems is that Krauss just makes the story about how the pieces fit together horribly boring.  I must also advise potential buyers and readers that there is a tremendous content issue, as well.<br /><br />The primary issue with the content can be seen in chapter nine, where Krauss shows his hand, indicating his motivation for writing the book: to establish ideological grounds that contradict, not just theologians, but philosophers.  The problem is that, after presenting the data in a disorganized way, explaining why there probably was a Big Bang, Krauss jumps (metaphysical) shark, basically demanding that the reader accept his bizarre definition of \\"nothing.\\"  In chapter ten, he defines nothing as something, which happens to be unstable.  I am quite ashamed that Dawkins backs up this line of thinking in the afterword, asserting that it is not \\"question-begging,\\" which is a boldfaced lie.  On top of this, we get conflicting facts about \\"nothing\\" from Krauss.  He says \\"under the right conditions\\" nothing can yield something, all the while maintaining that nothing always yields something.  The conditions that bring about these conditions, that is, the state that precedes the non-unique initial state(s) is what is of prime philosophical interest to philosophers and philosophers of physics.  This book is among the biggest wastes of money and time I have ever encountered, and it bothers me that there are a couple of us physicists that subscribe to views as presented in this book.  This book is emblematic of the kind of dogmatism that has lately arisen in the sciences, and which should be avoided at all costs.  Above all, a good scientist should adhere to the facts, and not go beyond the facts --a fatal flaw in this book.<br /><br />Since I often pick on scientists for making bizarre errors when they try to write histories of science or philosophies of science, I feel it necessary to add one, here.  Krauss seems to think Lemaitre originated the idea of the Big Bang, but it was Russian mathematician, Alexander Friedman, who was working on solutions to Einstein's equations.  I am pretty sure an article on this appeared in one of the American Physical Society's primary publications a little while ago, which makes this error the most bizarre of the ones found in the book: it suggests Krauss doesn't pay attention to APS publications.  I think Krauss would do much better in writing future books if he didn't try to do history and philosophy of science, worked on his writing style, and paid a bit more attention to the publications in his own profession. 184766895X|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo1J5QDAZPEZNG1]]<br /><br />NOW IN A NEW UPDATED EDITION WITH ACCOMPANYING CD<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />Commercial lawyers will certainly bless this book, now in a new third edition via Bloomsbury Professional.  The beauty and of course the utility of the book -- as the title indicates -- is that the clauses -- boilerplate clauses to be precise -- are listed for ease of use in alphabetical order from `acknowledgements' to `warranties'.<br /><br />This feature alone is a boon to the busy lawyer, or indeed anyone tasked with drafting a commercial contract. (One of the better known clauses in this alphabetical list is `completion' pertaining to the sale of real property.)<br /><br />But what exactly are `boilerplate clauses?'  Although the term generally defies precise definition, the co-authors Mark Anderson and Victor Warner state that it's used broadly to mean contract terms that are found in commercial agreements, almost irrespective of the subject matter of the agreement. Usually found toward the end of an agreement, they quite often are not necessarily concerned with its subject matter, or its main purpose.  Nonetheless, as the authors imply, you ignore or dismiss such clauses at your peril.<br /><br />Failure to consider (or understand) all the provisions of a commercial agreement (even the \\"boring \\"ones)' [i.e. boilerplate clauses] say the authors, can have serious consequences - hence the value of this book, which also examines a selection of other contract clauses frequently encountered in many types of commercial agreement.<br /><br />This new third edition has been written as a practical guide (rather than specifically a legal textbook or casebook) for practitioners frequently faced with the challenges posed by boilerplate and other commercial clauses in their everyday commercial work.  Following on from the two previous editions, this new one provides additional explanations and commentary on the legal principles which underline a boilerplate clause.<br /><br />Other new material includes sections on data protection, freedom of information and good faith and much more besides.  The book also provides extensive tables of cases, statutes, and statutory instruments, plus an appendix which features a sample boilerplate agreement and detailed index.<br /><br />Primarily this is a useful practitioner guide to an often confusing, yet crucially important area of law, although the authors are careful to state that it is not a general guide to negotiating or drafting contracts.  For that they recommend their book `Drafting and Negotiating Commercial Contracts' also published, like this particular volume, in 2012.planations and commentary on the legal principles which underline a boilerplate clause.<br /><br />Other new material includes sections on data protection, freedom of information and good faith and much more besides. Letters from Zedelghem: 3 stars: Again, we have a light hearted witty funny little story to begin, turned into a total disaster and tragedy by the end.  I'm not sure if the little light at the end of the tunnel is so great in this one.  I felt this story above all, had the most hopeless ending, but at no point did I ever like or identify with the main character Frobisher, so his nasty end didn't greatly impact me.<br /><br />3. Half-Lives: The First Luisa Rey Mystery:  5 stars: This is where the book really shines in my opinion.  And here we start to see the fundamental shift of things starting off badly, and ending well.  I felt like this truly was the novel Cloud Atlas, and all the other stories just feed into this one great story.  I really liked the characters and related to them, and I liked the \\"murder mystery spy novel\\" approach to it.  This story just felt good all around, and to be honest, was the only thing that kept me reading this entire novel from front to back.  I still feel that the author perhaps would have been better off just expanding this story into a full novel.  I think that novel would have been great.<br /><br />4. The Ghastly Ordeal of Timothy Cavendish: 2 stars: This one was hard, I LOVED the beginning, and wanted so much to like the ending, but it was just so long and dragging at the end, that I started skimming and just wanted it to end.  I love the british style humor, and most of the characters were great, and I love the idea of being trapped in an institute and making a daring escape.  Kind of like a feel better version of One Flew Over the Cukoos Nest.  But the ending still killed this one, and ended up as the most disregardable of the 6.  Pity.<br /><br />5. An Orison of Sonmi~451: 1 star:  Total crap.  From beginning to end, I couldn't see the point of this story.  I didn't like the interview style narrative, could NOT get into the characters, nor could I even visualize this Sonmi as any kind of messianic character.  I'm sure that buried deep in this story is some kind of profound story, but it completely lost me.  This was the worst of the 6 for me, and only served to add more pages of bloat to this novel.<br /><br />6. Sloosha's Crossin' an' Ev'rythin' After: 3 stars:  Here is what the author intended to be the center of the story I'm sure.  But compared to Luisa Rey, I feel that the average person will overlook this story due to its odd characters and really abrasively written narrative.  The story here is rich and compelling, but the writing style, particularly the harsh dialect used to tell this amazing story is so fragmented and off-putting, that its as if the author himself sabotaged this story at birth.  Where is it written that peoples of the far future must talk a stupid garbled english that's impossible to read?  Why could this story not have just been written plain?  I have no clue, but it wrecked the presentation for me.<br /><br />So, there we have it.  What I felt held up and brought down the central concept of this novel.  The novel is good, the writing is great, most of the characters are fun, but ultimately, the novel still lacks more than it accomplishes.  But, to be fair, it was an ambitious project, and its easy of me to knit pick it in hind sight from a far, because I have those advantages.  I give Mitchell credit for having this great idea for a novel, and trying his best to present it, but I think he needed help bringing it all together, and from what I've heard, perhaps the movie actually does this a bit better than he was able too.  I shall see.?  I have no clue, but it wrecked the presentation for me.<br /><br />So, there we have it.  What I felt held up and brought down the central concept of this novel.  The novel is good, the writing is great, most of the characters are fun, but ultimately, the novel still lacks more than it accomplishes.  But, to be fair, it was an ambitious project, and its easy of me to knit pick it in hind sight from a far, because I have those advantages.  I give Mitchell credit for having this great idea for a novel, and trying his best to present it, but I think he needed help bringing it all together, and from what I've heard, perhaps the movie actually does this a bit better than he was able too. 0316228532|4.0|In spite of reading negative reviews ahead of time, many people who attempt to read the Casual Vacancy are probably hoping that they will have an experience with it comparable to their enjoyment of the Harry Potter books. Where it was hard to put the Harry Potter books down because we liked the characters so much, it is hard to keep reading The Casual Vacancy because we like the characters so little. What was fascinating to me was just how different the two worlds are that Rowling has created. Harry Potter is about good vs. evil, friendship, loyalty, self-sacrifice,  and survival on a grand scale.  The Casual Vacancy is about the life of a small village of no particular importance filled with dysfunctional people who are destroyed.  Most of the characters are petty and self-absorbed, doing what they think they need to do to survive regardless of the havoc it causes for someone else. There is shocking meanness in the book. There is disturbing, raw vulgarity and exploitative sex. There is no love, and almost no charity. Yet, to my surprise, I cried at the end.  The Casual Vacancy is in many ways the antithesis of the Harry Potter books.  As such it shows Rowling to have quite a range as a writer. I didn't enjoy reading The Casual Vacancy the same way I enjoyed reading Harry Potter, but I'm glad I read it.  It was thought-provoking.  There are 3, maybe 4, women who are admirable in the book,who try to help others.  I think they will be what sticks with me, plus Rowling's point that it is easy to judge a person's actions but hard to understand them if you don't know their secrets or motives for acting.

B007K3JCAE|4.0|The producers of \\"A Game of Shadows\\" must think so, because Holmes has a problem worthy of James Bond -- preventing a world war (circa 1891). Holmes has become an action hero, whose skill at scientific fighting enables him to defeat any attacker. Even two. Hiding in the rafters.<br /><br />This is one of those rare sequels that's better -- much better (That's a joke. Get it?) -- than the original. The writing is sharper, there's a lot less of Rachael McAdams, and the story goes beyond being a clever mystery to examining (if only superficially) why humans engage in war. I particularly enjoyed Jared Harris as Moriarty, who doesn't drool and slobber like the stereotypical movie villain. The Bond/Grant train fight is reprised (though as such a bloated battle as to approach absurdity), and the auction scene from \\"North by Northwest\\" (sometimes considered a precursor of the Bond films) is winked at.<br /><br />Nevertheless, the four-star rating I gave the first film has to remain, simply because Holmes' character has been altered to near-unrecognizability. He's like the anthropomorphic Jiminy Cricket in \\"Pinocchio\\" -- he's a cricket because the other characters /say/ he is. Had Holmes remained as Conan Doyle created him, this film would have been a significant contribution to the Holmes tales. As it is, it is merely an entertaining confection.<br /><br />There are at least two anachronisms, one major, which I leave the viewer to discover. 0553386794|4.0|It has taken me three attempts, since 1996, to finish reading this book, but the delay isn't exactly George R.R. Martin's fault. The first two attempts were reading copies that belonged to other people, and I didn't have time to finish the book before I had to return it to its owner. After seeing previews of the HBO series, I purchased a copy and I must say I thoroughly enjoyed the book, with one proviso, which I will mention at the end of this review.<br />The strength of this book, and of this series to date, is Mr. Martin's ability to write many different characters, each with a different voice; and his ability to write an interweaving narrative and keep track of a seemingly infinite number of intertwining story lines. The characters are all interesting. The heroes of the story are all seriously flawed, and, unlike most fantasy villains, the bad guys are three dimensional and don't see themselves as evil, and often are just as engaging and endearing as the heroes of the tale.  As a case in point, Eddard Stark is brave and loyal, but blind to the ambitions of others, seeing everybody else as being as noble and loyal as himself, which costs him his live. His wife is a rather vindictive shrew, when all is said and done, unwilling to accept the flaws of others. King Robert is a cad, there's no other way to put it. Sansa Stark is an addlepated idiot. Only her younger sister Arya, seems to have the brains that God gave a goose. These are the good guys. On the other side of the ledger, we have a pair of incestuous twins with murderous hearts, a scheming father, and a misshapen dwarf whose virtues pose him on the brink of going over to the good side. The rest of the cast is made up of various hangers-on and lick-spittles who often play both ends against the middle as events unfold.<br />Each chapter serves up a portion of a story, told from the point of view of one character, and all are so well written that I found myself interested in each chapter and wanting to see how their particular portion of the story fits into the whole. In a genre of fiction that is dominated by stories that are, at best, retreads on the Theme of Tolkien, George R. R. Martin's novel is refreshingly unique, and though it's a long book, it doesn't bog the reader down with things like Elven languages and thousands of years of prehistory worked into the text.<br />And this brings me to the one real flaw of this book. I read it in the mass market paperback edition, and I will have to admit that due to my aging eyes, the print in the paperback is just too darned small. It was difficult to read this book without wearing my bifocals. This book should really have been split into two paperbacks with larger print, rather than using such a small font in order to cram the story into one volume. My advice to readers is too look for a larger trade paperback version, or the hardback version. Even if you have 20-20 vision, you'll probably need glasses by the time you finish reading the tiny, tiny print in the mass market paperback edition.w their particular portion of the story fits into the whole. I would be willing to bet that no other artist (outside of perhaps the classical genre) has ever done something like this - released an album full of the most beautiful and perfect tracks, so that one cannot help but love every single one of them.<br /><br />The first track, \\"Somewhere Over the Rainbow\\", is startling in its difference.  It is like a new song.  In Susan's plaintive and beautiful voice the lyrics have real, deep meaning and she transports the listener with her to the land where dreams come true.  In \\"Bring Him Home\\", her voice begins acapella and then the orchestra comes in to lend dramatic support for the remainder of the song. This is heartrendingly beautiful, sung with such intensity by this perfect female voice. Another of my all-time favourite songs is \\"All I Ask of You\\", but never before have I been so moved by it. Donny and Susan's two voices are perfect together. When hard things remain unspoken between people then the distance grows exponentially.<br /><br />One of the beautiful parts of this book is that it is about an ordinary man who decides to listen to himself & do something unusual for a friend dying of cancer. The story of his mistakes, success & determined choices is about his process along the way & how he changes and affects changes in others.<br /><br />He has tremendous resolve and he is terribly discouraged as anyone is who does something courageous. He decides to walk over 500 miles to his friend. It's the truth of this journey that captures the reader;  from the band aids (plasters) & sore feet including his denial that she'll recover because of his pilgrimage.<br /><br />As Harold walks he reflects a lot on his life. The actual tragedy is not revealed until the end. The way their silence piles up in impossible layers is the second tragedy that still might be repaired......or not, which is true of so many couples. It is powerful to read about the wife's struggle to think kinder thoughts, and not be able to squeak the words out because of her habit of anger.<br /><br />The purpose of anger is to create distance and says \\"I'm important.\\" It can be a very useful tool. Harold's wife demonstrates that a habit of anger is an entirely different story and leads to the lonely path of bitterness. I often caution those who start down this path because it is such an awful choice for the soul. Bitterness becomes a comforting prison that's hard to leave.<br /><br />Harold makes the mistakes of those who are kind to a fault. B004E2XAFW|5.0|There are the albums that get listened to once or twice, maybe ripped to the player storage, and then sort of disappear. There are the albums that get \\"put into the rotation\\" and get played a couple of times (maybe) a month.<br /><br />Then there are albums like this one. Ones that you listen to, and find yourself coming back from a dazed \\"OMG--what was THAT!?\\" state. And you turn up the volume another notch, and listen to it again.<br /><br />What's that you say? \\"Hey, I've listened to Mother Earth, and this doesn't sound like what I heard there.\\" Damn right it doesn't. Mother Earth (which, for the record, I also thoroughly enjoy) is the elegant Bohemian beauty next door, who occasionally shakes the flowers out of her hair and slips on a mini. The Unforgiving is Mother Earth's bad-girl half-sister, dolled up in spike heels and leather for a night of hard partying, and daring you to keep up.<br /><br />Track-by-track reviews don't do much for me, so we won't go here. And, for this album, I don't think it would add much: this whole album is a single wild train ride through the night, lights flashing, bells ringing, and whistle sounding. It slows down a little in a couple of places (\\"Fire and Ice\\", \\"Lost\\") to let you catch your breath, but don't be fooled: you'll be screaming over the next hill in a couple of minutes. If you want to get off at each stop and stroll around, you should have taken the bus.<br /><br />That said, it's worth mentioning the consistent high quality of the tracks. There are those legendary albums (Thriller, The Dark Side of the Moon, Zoso, etc.) that take a bunch of tracks that can easily be (and frequently were) massive single hits by themselves, and combine them into a whole that is greater than the sum of its parts. Obviously The Unforgiving won't achieve the legendary status and sales numbers of those blockbusters, but that's because of today's market and WT's place in that market. In the symphonicmetalrock intersection where WT now lives, The Unforgiving sets the bar. Of the 11 music tracks on the CD version, easily 7 could be smash singles.<br /><br />Of the 3 download-only tracks, only \\"I Don't Wanna\\" contends for a place on the album; the other two seem more like stand-alone one-offs. \\"The Last Dance\\" is a particularly odd duck; if someone had played it for me with the vocal dummied out on piano and asked me to guess who wrote it, I'd have probably said \\"Loreena McKennitt\\" (don't worry; that's a good thing).<br /><br />Sharon doesn't quite command the vocal tornado of a legendary rock goddess like, say, Pat Benatar in her prime; in a couple of places, such as toward the end of \\"Iron\\", it feels like Sharon is yelling the lyrics at you rather than singing them to you. But overall this is a powerful step up that ladder. On some past efforts (q.v. Mother Earth again), Sharon has had a tendency to get a little thin/reedy/borderline squeaky. Not here; she grabs this material with both hands, steps up with authority, and slams it down your throat. And you like it. You like it a lot. \\"Thank you, Ma'am; may I have another?\\"<br /><br />If there's any justice out there, The Unforgiving will do for symphonicmetalrock what Fallen did for gothicmetalrock: make people who hadn't even heard of the genre, much less the artist, sit up and say \\"Wow--how have I managed to overlook this amazing stuff?\\"<br /><br />So, if you like symphonic metal, you should by this album. Indeed, if you like rock music of any kind, you should buy this album.<br /><br />Heck, if you have ears that work, you owe it to yourself to listen to this one.The Unforgiving sets the bar. Of the 11 music tracks on the CD version, easily 7 could be smash singles.<br /><br />Of the 3 download-only tracks, only \\"I Don't Wanna\\" contends for a place on the album; the other two seem more like stand-alone one-offs. \\"The Last Dance\\" is a particularly odd duck; if someone had played it for me with the vocal dummied out on piano and asked me to guess who wrote it, I'd have probably said \\"Loreena McKennitt\\" (don't worry; that's a good thing).<br /><br />Sharon doesn't quite command the vocal tornado of a legendary rock goddess like, say, Pat Benatar in her prime; in a couple of places, such as toward the end of \\"Iron\\", it feels like Sharon is yelling the lyrics at you rather than singing them to you. But overall this is a powerful step up that ladder. On some past efforts (q.v. Mother Earth again), Sharon has had a tendency to get a little thin/reedy/borderline squeaky. Not here; she grabs this material with both hands, steps up with authority, and slams it down your throat. And you like it. You like it a lot. \\"Thank you, Ma'am; may I have another?\\"<br /><br />If there's any justice out there, The Unforgiving will do for symphonicmetalrock what Fallen did for gothicmetalrock: make people who hadn't even heard of the genre, much less the artist, sit up and say \\"Wow--how have I managed to overlook this amazing stuff?\\"<br /><br />So, if you like symphonic metal, you should by this album. Indeed, if you like rock music of any kind, you should buy this album.<br /><br />Heck, if you have ears that work, you owe it to yourself to listen to this one.
